## 71 - A Certain Elf’s Soliloquy 1

If my fading memory was correct, there would have been no particular reason.

One must never forget that they were a part of nature—taken on its own, that admonition made the Natural Harmony Committee not seem so bad.

We cannot live if separated from nature—I thought those were good words, and I believed they were true.

They preached coexistence with nature, founded an organization with harmony as its banner, and lived and died under a lofty ideal.

However, why the number of people increased who also “sought coexistence with monsters” from there remained a mystery to me.

There had been no special reason for me to join the Natural Harmony Committee.

I knew my parents were members of the organization, but I could say clearly that had not been my reason.

At most, my parents had only “kept their names on the roster” with that level of involvement, but for certain people with dangerous ideas, that sort of thing had not mattered in the least.

Dragged into a near-suicidal monster survey, my parents never returned.

Witnessing that tragedy, the reason I joined the Committee had not been “to prevent such a tragedy from ever happening again,” but simply “no particular reason.”

It had not been because I had some deep thought, nor because I had to find a way to live alone starting tomorrow.

Truly, there had been no reason.

As a result, my clan Forshna skillfully pulled the strings of the situation and succeeded in taking the reins of the Natural Harmony Committee.

By virtue of that achievement, I came to hold influence at a young age within my clan.

Then began the storm of marriage proposals.

I was fed up with the adults who gave bouquets to a child not even of age.

For the men whose eyes roved over my well-developed body, I felt nothing but disgust.

That was why I created a place to run away to—and that happened to be the Natural Harmony Committee; one never knows what life may bring.

Because my presence approached the Committee, various troublesome matters arose, but my standing as “the child who lost her parents in an incident caused by extremists” had a large influence.

As a result, I became someone close to the top of the Committee.

That said, my honest impression had been that I did not care about the Committee, so I wanted a suitable distance from it.

What helped me in that was the insane ideal of “coexistence with monsters.”

First, I posed the question to them: “Does there even exist a monster with which coexistence is possible?”

Naturally, it was a discussion that had been repeated many times before.

The crazy ones nodded as if it were only natural.

“Of course. Otherwise we would not make such a claim,” they all said in unison, and as I remembered, I said this to them:

“Then bring that monster before me. If coexistence is possible, it must understand our language, or at least have the will to try. Binding it with rope is utterly unacceptable. Bring it to me walking at your side like a friend.”

One side merely accommodating the other is not what you call coexistence.

By emphasizing that point and showing some understanding of their ideals, I kept my distance from the average members.

Before I realized it, I had become a central figure of the Committee, and even I could only clutch my head wondering, “How did it come to this?”

By that time, much time had passed since I became an adult, and I had become someone relied upon; I had enough pride to know I could move people, even counting among the most influential within my clan Forshna.

The number of men proposing to me never decreased, but I simply could not feel that way toward anyone.

Apparently, I had become someone who could not rid herself of disgust or wariness toward adult men and simply could not accept them.

It had not been with the intent of overcoming that, but I came to look after children.

They were children at an age of excessive mischief, but when they were happy to see a smile return to my face, I realized I had lacked any emotional room before.

Since then, I began to prioritize time with the children and even played at being a teacher.

It was good that I smiled more—enough to notice it myself—but there was a little too much physical contact from the boys.

I laughed it off, thinking it might help me overcome my dislike of men.

Then one day my underwear was stolen.

Even I got angry at that, but apparently the boys did not know the meaning of “learning a lesson,” as they became even more eager to target my underwear.

‘Very well, if that’s your little game, I’ll take you on,’ and each day became a contest of wills with the children.

I thought if I made them truly understand the awe-inspiring power of adults, excessive mischief would subside.

The result was a crushing defeat.

Day after day, I was at the mercy of the children who tried every trick to strip me.

It was then that a grim report was delivered.

The revival of the “Forest’s Nightmare”—the monster that had devoured countless elves once again appeared alive before us.

As the Council of Sages discussed countermeasures, I felt a little lonely at losing my time with the children, but the problem ended in an unexpected way.

News arrived that the “Nightmare” had been devoured by another monster.

Apparently, there had been talk of preparing a sacrifice and driving it away, but to think some monster ate that abomination even the Sword Saint Schvard could not finish off—what kind of monster had appeared?

With a new threat to the village, those with positions within the clan were busy again in the Council of Sages.

I was among them, but I thought the most we could do for now was prepare for an attack; however, most in the Council were optimistic that it was not a “Nightmare” against which magic would not work.

Yet, to what extent magic would work on that monster remained unknown, and I thought we should not draw conclusions lightly.

Worried for the children, I returned once to confirm evacuation sites and security arrangements.

I had steeled myself that I would be busy for some time after that, but then I overheard something unbelievable.

It was about the monster that had devoured the “Nightmare,” but the problem was its content.

“They revived the forbidden magic of domination and brought the ‘Nightmare’ under control.”

“Who in the world would do such a thing?”

The answer was “Kirishia Reibern”—the survivor of that clan once called magic-mad.

She was a convincing enough figure, but when I learned she had summoned the eldest son of Zesato, I rushed out.

Zesato and Reibern had no small amount of bad blood.

I had to do something before a rash act occurred.

In the end, it was settled by tossing the whole matter to the Council of Sages, which was fine, but apparently Lady Kirishia still believed a woman of Forshna stole her man.

She did not think it was because she only ever buried herself in magic and neglected her man; the Reibern blood—no, the Reibern curse—was a troublesome thing.

I ordered members of my clan to keep watch on the new monster “Argos,” and I went to check on the children.

Just as she said, there was no doubt the crisis had passed.

All I could do was pray this peace would continue.

Busy days continued for a while after that.

I took every precaution and considered how to respond if Argos ran wild.

I showed my face at the Council of Sages, but the discussions were the same as always.

Was it a sin for having revived a forbidden magic, or should she be rewarded for preventing the village’s crisis?

Time passed without anyone reaching an answer.

During that time, the aged representatives who had been absent for a while appeared, perhaps surprised there was still no conclusion.

They watched the meeting for a while and repeatedly asked about what had transpired.

Then the faces of those usually noisy long-living ones changed.

“Forbidden magic is unacceptable. No matter how great the threat, if we touched it, the meaning of why we fought the Empire is lost, and even our cause is gone. Remember why we paid such a price and burned the Empire.”

At the heavy words of the elder Odel, those who had survived that era nodded.

But that did not mean a conclusion was reached. The opinions of the three were taken seriously, yet it still took some time before a decision, and in the end the head of Zesato pressed through with “We cannot leave forbidden magic unchecked.”

It would still be some time before people were sent to Lady Kirishia, but I breathed a sigh of relief, thinking, ‘This will be the end.’

Argos would likely be killed while still under domination.

Reports said he was not being fed properly; I approached several times to check, but Argos only silently watched me, as if observing.

They said it was “forbidden magic that robs even the will of its victim”—if that were true, then was his gaze only my imagination?

No matter the outcome, I understood Argos had no future, and I visited several times, at least to distract him from his hunger, bringing him fruit.

Whether it settled in his stomach, I could not say, but that was all I could do.

However, this act would later change the way I lived.

At the time, everyone believed Argos was under domination and thus safe.

Myself included—

+++

## 72 - A Certain Elf’s Soliloquy 2

The decision on Lady Kirishia’s punishment was made, and the brief grace period passed in the blink of an eye.

When she was brought into the ritual chamber, her face looked somewhat haggard, but a clear will could be read in her eyes.

Her attitude might have been submissive, but she was aiming for something.

As the Ritual of Binding solemnly proceeded, Lady Kirishia suddenly raised her voice and began to struggle violently.

Taking advantage of the loosened restraints from her prior compliance, she shook off the soldier gripping her arm, toppled a desk, and moved cleverly to avoid being surrounded while obstructing the soldiers’ movements.

“You fools! Did you really think I wouldn’t have made duplicates of the magic tool used for giving orders!?”

At her words, I ordered the nearby soldier to restrain her, but it was already too late.

“Come, Argos!”

At her shout, Argos burst through the ceiling.

What we had feared came to pass; Argos blocked our way, sealing off our movements.

Even when the waiting soldiers rushed in, the situation hardly improved. Lady Kirishia wore a triumphant grin and spewed her lofty rhetoric.

And then, for some incomprehensible reason, she gave the worst possible command:

“Argos, violate that woman.”

Yet Argos showed no sign of moving.

At that point, Zesato’s eldest son muttered one line:

“…Isn’t that one not female?”

Looking closely, there was no sign of any genitalia between its legs. It turned out Argos was not a “he.”

But without reproductive organs, it was neither male nor female—was it what you’d call “asexual”?

If so, perhaps Argos was some kind of mutated variant.

I never imagined I would encounter such a variant alive, but Lady Kirishia, undeterred, then ordered our deaths.

Everyone tensed at her words—and then Kirishia was slammed to the floor.

“What are you doing!? It’s them! Move this hand away, now!”

She struggled even while pinned down, but Argos ignored her. Instead, he searched through the scattered items on the floor for a pen. Finding one, he began writing on the overturned desk.

“You are no longer needed.”

I didn’t immediately understand the meaning.

But the next words he wrote made everyone present comprehend:

“Language and magic learning completed. Thank you for never doubting your magic’s success.”

Argos had merely been using her.

(No, the shocking part isn’t that! Less than forty days and he’s mastered the elven language to this level!?)

If Argos’s words were to be believed, he had completed his study of language and magic.

A monster that devoured even the “Nightmare,” possessing intelligence that could only be called abnormal—what were we supposed to call such a being?

Argos then effortlessly killed Lady Kirishia.

We all knew this wasn’t the end of the matter.

And we all knew who had to step forward.

I took a step and faced Argos.

“Why did you kill Kirishia—kill her?”

I already had a guess, but I needed to confirm it.

“Research subject and experimental animal. Life threat. Reason to kill.”

The phrasing was a bit clumsy compared to earlier, but that was how I interpreted it.

Zesato’s eldest son seemed to interpret it similarly and asked Argos why he waited until now to kill her.

“Resistance to domination’s effect required time. Life threat came before resistance possible.”

With that answer, we understood.

The domination magic Kirishia had revived had been incomplete—otherwise it should have been impossible to escape the control of a forbidden spell that powerful.

(Still, there’s also the possibility that domination magic simply didn’t work on Argos. But we could never make that public. If word spread that such a monster lived nearby, panic would be inevitable.)

Apparently, the same conclusion had occurred to others; I heard a voice behind me say,

“So it wasn’t failure but incompletion…”

To immediately take control of the situation by framing it as “the revived domination magic was incomplete”—I couldn’t help but silently praise the Zesato clan.

But my role wasn’t over yet, nor could it end here.

“I understand your reason for taking her life. Then let me ask another. Why did you do all of this?”

Depending on how he answered, we could measure the threat level posed by Argos.

More than that, I wanted to know for myself, but if he possessed intelligence beyond our expectations, it wasn’t hard to imagine how certain members of the Committee would react.

We needed to know for that reason as well.

But the answer that came back was: “Don’t understand intent of question.”

In other words, he was saying:

“Is that really something I need to answer?”

This was quite a bad sign.

I heard a small tongue click from behind; apparently others understood as well.

As things stood, Argos didn’t have any good impression of elves.

That was only natural, but we had hoped that with his level of intelligence, hostilities could be avoided.

From his wording, that hope now seemed slim.

So I changed my question, assuming the worst-case scenario.

“Why did you learn our language, our magic?”

Argos said nothing.

He showed signs of thinking, so he was likely searching for the right words.

I waited.

But the answer that came back was the worst one possible:

“Amusement.”

In other words, while resisting Kirishia’s domination magic, he learned the elven language just for fun.

If it had been out of interest toward us, that would have been one thing. But if he could learn language and magic just as a game, then there was no room for elves to intervene in his affairs.

Silence hung in the chamber with the gaping hole in its ceiling.

Any thought of possible exchange had to be wiped clean; now our priority had to be how to handle the threat named Argos.

I shifted my thinking and tried to speak again, but Argos leapt out through the hole in the ceiling.

If he left just like that, everything would end unresolved.

Driven by a nightmare-like dread, I ran out without attendants.

I chased him, but there was distance.

After running for a while, Argos stopped, and I seized the chance to leap and close the gap at once with wind magic.

“Where are you going? If I’m with you, I can take you most places within the village. If you’d like, shall we go together?”

Landing before Argos, I rattled the words off quickly.

If he left now with nothing achieved, everything would become irreparable.

Shoved forward by that fear, I stepped up.

Argos reached his hand toward me.

I couldn’t resist—no, I mustn’t.

He silently lifted me and set me on his shoulder.

The worst-case scenario could still be avoided.

Believing that, I let him silently carry me to wherever he was headed.

The place he took me was Lady Kirishia’s laboratory—when I asked what he intended to do there, the answer was an unwelcome one: “complete destruction.”

I had been prepared to accommodate his demands as much as possible to prevent hostility with the elves, but I hadn’t expected him to ask me to help burn it, even if it was outside the village.

He resorted to force when I hesitated, and I had no choice but to take the role of setting the fire.

Though it was in this form, I was nonetheless shocked that I ended up breaking the law.

As I reeled from that, Argos showed me the words he wrote on the ground:

“No reason to come here anymore.”

He was probably trying to erase information about himself as an intelligent being.

Was there something he didn’t want known?

Or, just as we were wary of him, was he also wary of us?

Thinking that lit a faint hope of understanding his behavior so far.

“What is your goal?”

If so, then he had taken risks to protect himself from the elven race.

“What do you intend to do with us?”

With such high intelligence, perhaps he couldn’t endure being ignorant.

“…What do you want from the elves?”

If that were true, how would Argos—a being with no others of his kind—find peace of mind?

(If he even has the concept of “loneliness”…)

As that thought crossed my mind, I lowered my gaze and saw new words he had written:

“Desire more fruit.”

Those words made me feel I understood a little about what kind of being he was.

Next time we met, we would talk longer.

There was no need to fight.

No need to forcibly close the distance either.

A relationship as neighbors living side by side would be just right.

I was sure that was the meaning behind those words.

The fire blazed higher, rising into the sky.

“Ah, how will I even begin to explain this…”

My small soliloquy didn’t reach the one beside me.

+++

## 73 - A Certain Former Shrine Maiden Wants To Slack Off

I survived.

Yes, I managed to survive.

Since I splendidly returned alive from the so-called shrine maiden role, which was practically just a human sacrifice, I ought to happily bask in praise.

And yet the members of the clan treated me like some kind of leper.

Apparently, the recluse from Zesato was in a foul mood, and the backlash was falling on me, but that wasn’t my problem.

If anything, I wanted to clap my hands and loudly shout, “Serves you right!”

For a while, I had been detained for questioning, but since I could laze around doing nothing, it wasn’t such a bad arrangement.

While those who witnessed that incident were still barred from leaving, somehow it became accepted that the so-called “Nightmare” threat had left, but the truth was different.

That “Nightmare” had been eaten.

I had been ordered to keep silent about it, but that didn’t matter to me.

If they wanted to shut my mouth, then they’d better cough up some compensation, right?

Hinting as much got me detained again.

“I think there’s such a thing as the right to know the truth.”

When I asserted this, I was accused of “needlessly trying to stir up chaos,” and my detention was further extended.

My lounging time was prolonged again, but this time I was released quickly.

So I barged into the village’s meeting room and demanded payment for having been forced to serve as the shrine maiden this time.

No one acknowledged the validity of my claim, and I was detained for the third time.

I vented my complaints about this matter to the familiar-looking, serious guard, and he begged me, “Please, just be quiet and stay still.”

There were some people who could be reasoned with.

I decided to grant his request, but when idle time dragged on for too long, even this scenery became boring.

More than anything, I lamented my misfortune, wondering where I should aim this anger, but he wouldn’t even look at me.

I see, my body was simply too attractive for him to focus on his duties properly.

Being too earnest could also be a problem.

Unfortunately, he didn’t even make it onto my list of potential lovers.

No matter how much one flaunted earnestness and sincerity, there were limits to everything.

It came time to part ways with this otherwise promising man, but I didn’t look back on the past.

Except for that woman. I’d never forgive her.

Every day I plotted revenge, wondering what I could do to her, but none of my ideas were realistic.

It seemed impossible to single-handedly take on someone from the influential Zesato clan.

You had to recognize when you couldn’t do something.

At least I understood that much.

Anyway, while I was burning up my energy on such personal matters, the village grew unusually noisy.

Apparently, someone from “Reibern,” the one even called magic-mad, had caused some incident.

I didn’t know who did what, but in these times, for something to get more attention than my case—wasn’t that a bit excessive in information control?

It seemed the Zesato lot desperately wanted the matter of appointing a shrine maiden on their own authority to fade away.

In that case, I had an idea of my own.

So I took action—but before I knew it, I was detained again.

For some reason, I was once more dressed in a shrine maiden outfit, bound hand and foot, and being escorted.

I didn’t understand what was happening.

Also, how long did they plan to keep making a frail maiden walk?

I complained, but no one replied.

Even I could tell how bad it looked to have one bound, exceptional beauty among a bunch of men.

So I tried not to provoke them and asked under whose orders they were acting—and discovered the worst possible thing.

Apparently, I was to be sacrificed to the monster that had eaten that “Nightmare.”

I couldn’t grasp what they meant and asked for details. It seemed the monster had been placed under control with the forbidden domination magic, but since the magic was incomplete, it regained freedom and now resided in this forest.

As an apology, they were going to hand me over to that monster.

“What? Don’t screw with me.”

I blurted it out before I could stop myself; nothing I could do now.

Continuing the discussion, I learned that damned woman had forcefully pushed this through and that these men couldn’t refuse even though they too would be endangered.

They even said there was a possibility everyone present would be killed.

“Eh? You’re kidding, right?”

All of them sighed with grim expressions and silently proceeded into the forest.

Maybe my nonstop talking annoyed them, because they gagged me with a rope.

Now I couldn’t even call for help.

Apparently, my life was about to end here.

Given this difference in numbers, I couldn’t tell what they might do if I resisted, so I just silently let myself be dragged along.

I thought there might still be a chance if that damned woman had overreached, so I struggled, but they easily pinned me to the ground, immobilizing me.

And then the feared situation happened.

“Hey, don’t you think it’s a waste to give this one to the monster?”

The men’s hands reached out, grabbing my clothes and tearing them.

The cloth covering my chest was the first to go, their gazes fixed on me. When I saw one man pulling down his pants, I struggled desperately, but the difference in strength was obvious, and my clothes were shredded.

There was no way I could throw off the man straddling me. Thinking I’d be violated and killed here, tears streamed down my face.

But salvation came.

The monster these men intended as their goal appeared.

I realized I had no way to escape even as I thought, “I’m saved,” so I prayed that these idiots would be slaughtered horribly, but they babbled out their mission and fled in a panic.

There had been some rapid-fire questions and answers, which revealed an astounding fact: this monster named Argos understood the elven language.

Since words could reach him, my wish bore fruit; realizing my life wasn’t in danger, having survived both mortal and chastity crises, anger welled up.

I screamed in my rage, never imagining a monster would scold me for it.

He seemed far more rational than I thought.

Anyway, after he untied me and I fixed my clothes, I double-checked to make sure he wouldn’t eat me. He poked my chest and said there wasn’t much to eat.

Soft, sure, but it’s not food!

At least I was reassured I wouldn’t be eaten, and my emotions exploded.

I didn’t know how long I vented my complaints, but apparently they were persuasive enough for even a monster to sympathize.

“Don’t overthink it; they’re just jealous,” he said—quite perceptive for a monster.

In other words, he understood I was attractive.

I couldn’t help praising myself, but then I recalled that this monster had saved my life before.

I thanked him for that, and he looked at me with a strangely puzzled expression, as if surprised.

In any case, since I’d be returning alive, it was time for revenge.

Just you wait, damned woman and all you men—I’ll make sure you learn exactly whose wrath you’ve incurred!

And now, I was at Argos’s dwelling.

He advised that if I returned before the village learned of that damned woman’s misdeeds, I might be silenced. Since I couldn’t possibly survive alone in this goblin-filled forest, I ended up tagging along with Argos.

But I never imagined he lived in Imperial ruins… I smelled a chance to make some money.

It seemed my luck was finally turning. Between the shrine maiden affair and now, things kept improving when this monster was around.

Perhaps it was fate?

Too bad, though—no matter how strong and lucrative he might be, he was still a monster.

If he were an elf… or a human, I’d have already seduced him with this body.

Still, the way he kept touching my chest whenever I got excited was not okay.

It seemed he used it as some kind of calming method, but wasn’t he treating me a bit too roughly?

But more important than that were the ruins.

I never thought such a place would be underground.

And perhaps understanding my frailty, Argos removed dangerous things for me.

Mhm, good work.

The long-awaited exploration of the underground ruins turned out to be terrible.

The phrase “I don’t even want to remember it” fit perfectly.

I never wanted to go to the back of those ruins again.

It seemed to be some kind of experimental facility—just what had they used such monsters for? The smell of money grew stronger, but Argos warned me it was dangerous.

Since he had saved my life and bore little hostility toward me, he spoke with me.

And not just Elvish—he was fluent in Furon as well; quite a versatile monster.

When I brought up Furon, he seemed interested in the Council and asked me all sorts of questions.

Very well, ask me anything.

Then my life with Argos began, but it ended sooner than I thought.

It had been three days rich with gains for me, and it pained me to think it might all go to waste.

More than that, I worried about whether I’d really be safe, so I asked Argos, never imagining that the name of Forshna, one of the Four Great Clans, would come up here.

“This guy has connections too?” I thought, all the more regretting he wasn’t of a humanoid race.

Anyway, under the Forshna clan, I could slack off safely.

He provided meals, though the food here was monotonous.

The sleeping quarters weren’t bad, but there were remains of that monster deep in the ruins, so I was too scared to sleep far from him.

Thus, I didn’t have much attachment to staying here.

I’d have liked to bring something back if possible, but this lab coat alone should be enough to give credibility.

Riding on Argos’s shoulder, we set off!

I thought I’d have to endure this awkward ride all the way to the village, but it seemed our target person was by the river, so I could get off sooner than expected.

Ah, heaven truly favored me.

Or rather, loved me?

As long as the Forshna clan protected me and I testified as needed, I could slack off until this incident wrapped up.

On top of that, I’d even earn their gratitude—perfect.

That was what I thought—but the look the Forshna woman gave me was harsh.

No, the atmosphere itself was extraordinary.

(Huh? Argos-san, this isn’t what you said would happen, is it?)

+++

## 74

I had known it, but the Empire’s territory was vast.

The continent was divided into north and south, and the Empire’s land stretched all the way down to the narrowing section like a constricted waist.

It boasted the largest land area on the continent, north to south combined, so naturally traveling from one end to the other took time. The Empire solved this problem by using railways.

With trains and vehicles as means of transportation, one could traverse almost the entire continent in less than a day. Needless to say, this advantage elevated the Empire to the world’s greatest superpower. Now, unable to enjoy that benefit, I vented my sorrow on a convenient ogre.

(Seriously, if only there were even railway tracks left! Then I could travel so much easier!)

After finishing the ogre—who had become my punching bag—with a one-two punch, I exhaled deeply and sat on a convenient rock.

It was already the third day since I had left the main base. Looking up at the sky was difficult in this dense forest; only faint gaps allowed a view, and the gloom in broad daylight was getting to me a little.

Being in the same environment for too long wasn’t good.

Originally, the southern region of the Empire had lagged behind in development, with many lands rich in nature.

(No, perhaps it’s more accurate to say many areas couldn’t be developed due to geographical reasons?)

Especially the southwest, left almost untouched partly because of its proximity to elven territory, and the presence of a small mountain range meant no need to station troops for defense. As a result, this area had become known as one of the Empire’s prime hunting grounds.

Given the demand, the authorities couldn’t ignore the voices of their citizens and push for development. Instead, they decided, “Let’s just make it a hunting ground,” creating a rare monster-breeding preserve.

In other words, I was traveling almost directly south from the Empire’s western region near the elven lands, moving through this man-made paradise.

Let me be honest:

(There’s no way I could remember such a niche leisure facility!)

It was only upon seeing a decayed sign that I recalled its existence, which confirmed that monster hunting had definitely been a minority hobby.

After all, battles with giant creatures were thrilling enough inside games—said the rather large monster himself.

Anyway, after lowering the ogre—which no longer made a sound—from the tree, I left the area.

Charging straight south would mean encountering who knows how many monsters.

I couldn’t afford to stop and fight each time, so I decided to alter my course southeast.

I wasn’t well-versed in the geography of this area, but I vaguely remembered there being a decently sized city adjacent to farmland.

Given the bad roads that hindered travel speed, reaching that far would likely be my limit for the day.

With that thought, I changed my route, but it took longer than expected to find the ruins of the city; I ended up arriving late at night.

Truly, nothing ever went as planned.

The next morning, I wanted to explore the ruins, so I spent the night under any roof I could find. But calling it a bed was generous; it was so shoddy I hardly slept.

“Mmm, things don’t feel like they’re going well.”

I muttered as I explored the ruins of the nameless city.

And I discovered an unexpected fact.

(Yeah, a lot of stuff has been taken.)

There were traces that tools and supplies had been carried off, and the amount taken was significant judging from the city’s condition.

(If I recall correctly… goblins dominate the north. Orcs dominate the south, right?)

If the information I got from Number 6 was correct, the suspects were undoubtedly orcs.

With orcs’ level of intelligence, they could easily use obvious tools.

Unlike goblins, who only pillaged, orcs were known to have individuals in their groups smart enough to handle production.

(An orc group left alone for 200 years using Imperial resources—this might have gone beyond the level of a mere horde.)

On top of that, the Empire had maintained a large-scale agricultural district.

If the orcs had reused it, it wouldn’t be strange if they’d built an orc kingdom.

If my opponents were goblins, even tens of thousands wouldn’t be a problem, but with orcs… how would it turn out?

(Depends on their scale. And if by some chance any usable Imperial weapons remained, that would be frightening.)

Even high-quality metal tools alone could serve as weapons.

I doubted they could harm this body of mine, but if used for something else, I’d need to be cautious.

“I need to confirm it.”

I growled softly and left the ruins, heading toward where I remembered the farmlands being.

After about two hours of struggling with the rough terrain, I spotted smoke rising in the sky.

Just as the density of the trees began to thin, I made this discovery.

(If it’s a wildfire, give me a break. If orcs are using fire, even worse.)

Fire was the beginning of civilization.

If the orcs were using it, they might have developed some kind of culture.

I felt hesitation at killing intelligent beings, but since they were orcs, I revised my thoughts.

Goblins were like cockroaches on a national scale.

Orcs then were like rats.

Indeed, pest extermination was important.

Orcs abducted people sometimes, since they treated anything bipedal as a means of sexual release—there was no mercy to give them.

But first, I’d observe.

Even with what I already knew, the orc group could have grown to a scale unprecedented in human history.

Until I grasped some understanding of their strength, I had to move stealthily.

Better safe than sorry.

When the numbers reached beyond human experience, you never knew what might happen.

There was even a chance some orc mage had emerged as powerful as an elf.

Aiming for the smoke, I walked cautiously, and when my plan solidified—my view opened up.

It was a farm.

I stood frozen, looking down on it.

One glance revealed the cultivated fields spreading out before me—astonishing.

And orcs were using fire, roasting and eating something.

(BBQ in broad daylight, huh…)

Three orcs surrounded a large iron plate, happily devouring grilled meat and vegetables.

They looked to be enjoying a meal after work in a friendly atmosphere.

They drank water from slightly dirty bottles while eating the roasted food.

Sometimes they argued, “That’s mine,” but the three of them happily ate under the blue sky, grunting and laughing cheerfully.

It was such an ordinary scene that it almost felt mundane, and before I realized it, at my feet lay three enormous rats, blood-soaked and dead.

“I messed up.”

I tilted my head, stuck out my tongue, and tapped my head with my fist in a deliberately cute gesture.

Yeah, I got annoyed for some reason—couldn’t be helped.

(But seriously, to ignore my plan so suddenly and kill them… This emotion control function never works when it matters!)

What’s done was done; I left the corpses and snatched some vegetables before retreating.

Orcs had good noses, so hiding the bodies would be pointless.

Better not to bother in the first place.

Thus, stealth missions were pretty tough here, with even camouflage skills being of limited use.

I pondered, “Maybe I should just go guerrilla-style instead,” when I remembered:

(Oh right, my goal was to observe current Imperial citizens. Forget the orcs, ignore them.)

But this meant I’d have to detour around this farm, possibly running into their dwellings.

Well, I’d just cross that bridge when I came to it, I thought, heading back into the forest and resuming my journey.

As expected—or perhaps predictably—just around sunset, I discovered an orc settlement.

And it was terrifyingly huge. If I wanted to bypass it, I’d probably have to spend the night.

From the hilltop, I could see makeshift huts built from felled trees. The crude, oversized barn-like structures held a massive number of goblins tied up.

The intended use was obvious.

I checked just in case if there were any humans or elves… and of course, there were.

Two completely naked women, chained and motionless.

While I hesitated, wondering, “Should I save them?” I noticed something.

Their chests didn’t rise—they weren’t breathing.

(Too late… This isn’t something to be happy about, but honestly, I’m a little relieved.)

Judging by their condition, those two likely died today.

If I’d arrived earlier, I might have saved them.

But that was all “if.”

All I could do for the dead was kill the orcs, or keep watch to ensure no more women got captured—that was about it.

I considered taking their bodies back, but I was a monster now.

I wasn’t so irrational as to not consider how badly that could be misunderstood.

For now, I had to decide whether to cut through here or detour.

First, I needed intel, so I would gather it while hiding here.

I had to decide before the wind direction turned against me—the recent streak of bad luck was a bit concerning.

And just as I feared during my reconnaissance, an orc stopped, sniffing the air intently as if sensing something.

(Yeah, the wind shifted. Just as I thought. Damn it!)

+++

## 75

Several orcs within visible range had already reacted to my scent.

They didn’t seem to know what it was yet, but it was only a matter of time before they traced its source.

Retreating was an option, but that would make it look like I ran away from the orcs, and that didn’t sit well with me.

Still, staying up all night against an unknown number of orcs would be a hassle.

Unlike goblins, the smell wasn’t as strong, which was a relief, but overall their specs were much higher.

With their greater intelligence, they came up with tricks to escape, making them troublesome.

If they had a command unit, unlike goblins who never fled, the orcs would retreat depending on the situation to protect their King or Queen.

Therefore, they wouldn’t necessarily fight to the end like goblins.

So what should I do?

I didn’t want to retreat.

But fighting them would be a pain.

It was only a matter of time before I was found.

(Alright, I’ll just ignore the pigs and push straight through.)

I switched my thinking to breaking through head-on.

However, if I just ran across, it might look like I was avoiding a fight, which could embolden the orcs, and that wasn’t good.

So, I stood up and walked straight toward the horde of orcs.

When the orcs noticed me and raised their voices to alert the others, they all rushed toward the huts.

They were probably going to fetch weapons, so I ignored them and hopped over the shabby fence, stepping into their settlement.

My guess seemed right.

The orcs that came out of the huts carried spears and small shields.

But seeing orcs line up neatly in a row and wait their turn even while being invaded made for quite a surreal sight.

(And it’s a long line! Is that about 100 meters?)

While it was clear they were well-organized, their brains might have been a little lacking.

I ignored the armed orcs pointing their spears at me and lumbered right across the village.

By the way, orcs were about two meters tall, so they had to look slightly up at me.

Perhaps they realized I didn’t plan to do anything special, so they just surrounded me from a distance, not attempting any attacks.

I thought this might end without any trouble, but with that many of them, of course, there were bound to be some acting foolishly.

A particularly large orc blocked my path.

Big, but not ogre-sized—just big for an orc.

The halberd in its hands looked like it was made “for orcs,” perfectly sized for them, but it was too small for my proportionally larger hands, so I wasn’t interested.

It seemed annoyed that its kin were just watching the intruder without doing anything, squealing angrily while the others exchanged awkward glances.

Apparently being half-smart led to this.

I stared at it curiously, which seemed to offend it, as it raised its halberd to strike.

It seemed eager to die, so I granted its wish.

I pinched the blade of the halberd mid-swing to stop it, pulled the orc closer, and gave it a strong slap.

Its neck twisted halfway around to face backward, so it should be dead.

Now then, the reaction of pigs who saw an existence far above themselves—panic.

Apparently, this settlement had no leader despite their numbers, as the orcs began squealing and running around in terror.

The ones pointing spears from afar were the most composed; it seemed these orcs were genuine pigs living comfortably in a safe zone.

Not worth paying any mind.

Still, since I hadn’t intended to deal with them anyway, this worked out for me.

I continued striding arrogantly through the orc settlement, but no one stepped forward to stop me.

In the end, I made it out of the village without any real attacks.

The worst they did was throw stones, probably for intimidation, but even those dropped short before reaching me.

Now, as I continued heading south, what I saw here raised concerns about the orcs’ range of activity.

Judging from the location of the farms, this place should have served as a buffer zone… but while they showed trained movements, there was no sign they intended to fight.

In other words, they looked unprepared for combat.

Or perhaps it should be seen as “they didn’t need to be prepared.”

The latter was the troublesome one—if it were the former, it could just be dismissed as “the orcs were lazy,” but if it was the latter, there might be a large base elsewhere, or something equivalent, making this area a safe zone.

(In the worst case, there really might be an “Orc Kingdom.” And geographically, the ones dealing with it would be the Furon Council. The northern defenses are virtually nonexistent, but if their entire army is facing south, that would at least explain the weakness there.)

This might require some form of intervention.

I had planned to observe and possibly get involved with Furon depending on the situation, but… this case was unexpected.

For now, I continued south.

I had to see how far the orc influence extended, or it could hinder my future activities.

If push came to shove, I could just wipe them out.

But since they had finally grown crops on a large enough scale, I wanted to consider using them as a stable source of income.

There was no need to hold back against monsters.

When they abandoned farming, that would be the pigs’ end.

So, I spent the night moving south, but the situation was worse than I thought.

Why? Because the orcs had built a fortress.

And it was currently under expansion.

Working physical labor early in the morning—how admirable.

But orcs engaging in labor was not a good sign for humans.

This was probably their forward base, but given they were leisurely expanding it, the actual frontline was even further south.

(Isn’t the orcs’ sphere of influence a bit too wide? Depending on the situation east and west, they might have military strength on a national level.)

If the Furon Council had declined to the level of the old empire’s surrounding countries, they could very well be facing an existential crisis.

While groaning and watching the orcs stacking stones from a distance, I heard a human voice.

A woman’s voice—but not a scream.

The shouting coming from inside the fortress sounded as if she were fighting.

What in the world was going on? I grew curious, but since humans weren’t attacking the fortress, the situation clearly wasn’t good.

Depending on the case, a rescue might be in order.

I dropped my backpack and set it down somewhere, then approached the fortress from upwind, using my mimicry ability to climb the wall.

“You filthy pigs!”

Upon reaching the outer wall and seeing there were no guards, I almost laughed, but the sight before me wasn’t funny.

A naked woman holding a sword was fighting an orc wielding a spear.

The surrounding orcs clapped and laughed gleefully, while the orc fighting her grinned lewdly, easily dodging her clumsy swings.

There was no doubt she wasn’t used to handling a sword, and with a dulled shortsword, it would be difficult to land a fatal blow.

In short, this was just a show.

Not only were they tormenting captives, they were using them as toys.

It was safe to assume there were quite a number of women imprisoned in this fortress.

Otherwise, orcs wouldn’t seek out such entertainment.

Orcs were using humans as toys.

Orcs, of all creatures, were mocking those of imperial blood.

The moment I thought that, a switch flipped.

I regained my composure and looked down on the scene with a head gone cold.

(For now, all orcs will be slaughtered. That’s non-negotiable.)

The problem was how many humans were captured.

And what to do with them afterward—but that could wait until I rescued them.

I climbed back down from the outer wall, confirmed no one saw me, and deactivated my mimicry ability.

Then I climbed back up and revealed myself—but the orcs were so engrossed in the spectacle that none of them even looked my way.

No one even noticed me as I stood alone atop the outer wall.

(Alright, pigs, get ready.)

Kicking off the wall with enough force to break it, I leapt high into the air.

Finally, some of the pigs noticed, pointing and yelling in confusion.

I landed with a tremendous crash between the orc and the woman, raising a cloud of dust.

Now then, let the massacre begin.

+++

## 76

The first action I took was to move my tail and snatch the sword from the woman.

Even seen up close, the blade was indeed blunted, making it insufficient not only as a sword but even as a blunt weapon.

(If you’re going to hand over something like this to play with, I might as well deal with them myself using it.)

I slammed the short sword in my hand into the orc’s side.

Watching the orc, who dropped its spear and went flying, finally spurred the pigs to act against the intruder. However, since they could not fight without weapons, they scrambled indoors as if fleeing.

As for me, I leisurely walked over to the pig that had been blown away, pressed the short sword against the fallen orc’s neck, and slowly pushed it in with my body weight.

Naturally, since the blade was blunted, it did not pierce; instead, it crushed the flesh of the neck as the short sword sank in.

The pig struggled desperately to push it away, but there was no way an orc’s level of strength could lift the blade weighed down by my body. As its breathing grew labored, it began to pat my fingers holding the hilt.

I could have let it suffocate like that, but instead, I forced the sword in, shattering the bones of its neck.

This way, I could free my hands quickly, and it would suffer and die from being unable to breathe properly due to the bleeding.

Now then, there was something I had to do before the pigs returned.

Some of them brandished weapons to threaten me, but I ignored them and walked toward the gate.

There were three women currently in the hall—the one who had been fighting the pig earlier had joined the others, and they were hiding together in one place.

It was fortunate that at least one of them seemed able to think and act calmly.

I waited in front of the gate until the orcs with weapons had all gathered one after another.

Then, when an orc wearing a helmet appeared and raised its sword to give the order, the armed pigs charged toward me.

After confirming that, I closed the gate.

Strictly speaking, since it was an iron gate—quite audacious of them—I deformed it, making it impossible to open or close.

I spread my arms wide toward the halted group of orcs in a welcoming gesture.

(Welcome to hell.)

I wondered if my message of “No one escapes” had been conveyed?

No, there was no way it hadn’t been.

If it hadn’t, you lot would be on the level of goblins.

I leapt into the armed orc horde.

The massacre began.

A pig too slow to flee from my landing point was trampled flat.

A group of pigs with shields was swept aside by a single swing of my arm, sent flying through the air.

A thrusting spear snapped; an axe that had supposedly slashed broke.

Orcs who had taken up high positions shot arrows, but all of them bounced off without piercing my hard outer hide.

“Gaaaaaaaah!”

A charge—an all-out body slam—tore pig flesh and shattered bones.

I grabbed pigs and hurled them at orcs on the outer wall.

I hurled them at pigs inside the hall.

I stomped.

I punched.

I kicked.

With each action I took, a pig’s life was snuffed out.

Before long, there was nothing moving inside the fort.

When I crushed the head of a helmeted pig that had been playing dead, orcs pretending to be dead similarly began to flee.

However, since there was only one gate, their only escape was to jump from the outer wall, and I had piled pig corpses on the stairs leading up there during the earlier carnage, so climbing up would be no easy task.

The last one dropped to its knees as if begging for its life, rubbed its forehead against the ground, and raised its clasped hands over its head.

I mercilessly drove the spear I had picked up into its head.

Now, the ones outside remained.

They likely knew something abnormal was happening inside due to the commotion, but since they could not open the gate, they had no idea what was going on in here.

They could probably sense it from the smell of blood and the pigs’ screams, but that didn’t mean all of them would run.

Any pigs too slow to flee would all be slaughtered.

I would make them remember that they were the “prey.”

The number I killed inside and outside combined was less than 300.

The ones outside had not fled as much as I had expected, so it took me longer to exterminate them, but since I was able to deal with more pigs, I considered it acceptable.

Now then, next were the women inside… but first, I would approach them amicably.

During the cleanup, I had come up with a convenient scenario and wanted to proceed according to that setup.

First, I retrieved the backpack I had left before infiltrating.

Then, climbing back over the outer wall of the fort—I found a large number of nearly naked women.

When they saw me, they screamed, but one of them gave a firm order to quiet down, and they obediently complied.

(As expected, I should assume there’s a soldier mixed among them.)

That made things easier for me, so I welcomed it.

For now, I counted their numbers… there were as many as 38 of them.

As I descended the outer wall and slowly walked toward them, women holding orc spears blocked my path.

So I took out a notepad and pen from my backpack, scribbled some words, tore off a sheet, and thrust it before the woman in front.

“Shall we talk?”

The woman looked at the memo with a shocked expression and nervously took the paper held out to her.

“You… can understand our language?”

I nodded and wrote on the notepad again.

Since it was going to be a slightly long text, I had them wait. While remaining cautious, some of the women looked at me with interest.

It seemed they had not been captured by the orcs for long and still had the leeway to observe the situation.

Once I finished writing, I tore off the memo and handed it to the representative woman.

Its contents were something like this:

“The attack on the orcs was for my own reasons, so I will omit the details here. Due to the nature of my objective, I want to meet your commander. In other words, I have only one request for you: to arrange a meeting and discussion with your commander or someone equivalent. If you agree to this, I promise to return you all safely.”

The representative woman looked back and forth between the memo and me multiple times, her expression one of disbelief.

“So, in short, you want to use us as a bargaining chip?”

One of the women standing behind her glanced at the memo and voiced her impression.

That interpretation was fine by me, so I nodded.

Even if the reason was flimsy, as long as it worked as a “reason,” people would accept it.

If they accepted it, they would stop doubting me unnecessarily or taking strange actions, and things could proceed smoothly.

And crucially, the fact that I wanted to meet with the commander of the Furon Council, or someone corresponding to that, was true.

What I sought was information gathered on the human side.

It would be quicker and more accurate to get information from Furon, who were presumably actively involved, than for me to observe the orcs alone.

In this deal, they might think they were being “incidentally” saved, but for me, this was also the main goal.

To accomplish both while not closing the distance too much, such a disguise was necessary.

“I will give you time to think. You can give your answer after scavenging supplies inside the fort.”

The representative nodded at the additional memo and began issuing instructions to the other women.

As expected, being nearly naked was a problem.

If they put on clothes and armed themselves, they would regain some mental composure.

After waiting for a while in the now-empty fort plaza, armed women emerged from the building one after another.

The problem was that the weapons in their hands were stained with blood—and it wasn’t orc blood.

(Well, of course. This fort wasn’t built yesterday. Those captured for a long time were still inside—or rather, there were those who could no longer move.)

They could not leave behind those who had reached their limits physically, or perhaps mentally—or in the worst case, both.

It must have been an agonizing decision.

(A world not ruled by law really leaves a bad taste in my mouth.)

That said, the way of might making right was, in a sense, the same for humans.

Since both sides imposed their own principles on each other, there was no room for complaints.

All that remained was silent brute force.

“We have decided to accept your proposal. If we can return safely, we will arrange a meeting with our headquarters.”

I nodded at the representative’s words, feeling a twinge of guilt for forcing them to make such a resolution.

However, if I had taken out a magic potion there and given it to them, I would have provided them with more than necessary.

Creating unnecessary debts could eventually lead to entanglements.

(Having a monster’s body comes with more inconveniences than I expected.)

After confirming their intent, I walked to the gate.

To display my power, I blew apart the massive iron plate that had become an immovable chunk due to its deformation with a single punch.

If that could give them even a little reassurance, all the better… or so I thought, but when I looked back, their faces still looked grim.

Even after seeing this strength, there were still factors causing unease—so I steeled my mind, realizing my vigilance had unconsciously slackened.

The mass of iron that had been the gate fell to the ground with a loud crash.

It would be my first escort mission.

I would challenge it with all my strength, determined not to let a single casualty occur.

+++

## 77

As expected, walking with thirty-eight women slowed us down more than anticipated.

Or rather, my own sense of time had become completely distorted.

It was good that I had grown accustomed to my enhanced physical abilities, but I ended up making this mistake because I still retained human instincts in strange ways.

(Ah, can’t they move a little faster…?)

The group’s progress was slow as they moved while keeping watch on their surroundings, and no matter how much I told them, “I’ll take care of the perimeter surveillance,” they would not stop.

Thanks to that, we were barely making any progress, and the schedule had gone off the rails completely.

It wasn’t so much that they didn’t trust me; rather, they seemed driven by fear, leaving me with no way to do anything about it.

Thinking that I should distract them somehow, I decided to ask a few questions, and it turned out all of them were regular soldiers.

Their military branch was the musket corps.

I wondered, “If they had guns, why on earth did they end up as prisoners of some Orcs?” but they carried muskets, not rifles.

On top of that, they had been ambushed and hit on the flank; when the front line collapsed, those who failed to retreat in time were captured en masse and dragged to that fortress.

Apparently, nearly three hundred were captured in total, but with men turned into food and women made into playthings, only this many survived, enduring the humiliation of being sent to the rear and used as toys until I came along.

As for that “game” they mentioned, it seemed to follow the rule: “Win and you’re freed; lose and you become food.” But knowing Orcs, it went without saying what they would do before eating their victims.

Moreover, the ones eaten weren’t even the losers themselves but two women who happened to be in the hall, showing that Orcs had no shred of decency as living beings.

When I kept listening, many of them began to quietly weep, so I decided not to ask more about it, but I still had to hear information about the battlefield.

“From what we know, the Orc army numbers over ten thousand.”

As expected, it seemed that Orcs from across the former Fullretos Empire territory had gathered.

In that case, the upper limit of their numbers would depend on how much of the former empire’s farmland they were exploiting.

If they increased too much and had food shortages, they could have simply expanded their power northward.

Yet the fact that they took a southern route meant—clearly, humans were their target.

(This whole “Orc Kingdom” thing might not just be a joke anymore.)

If an army of Orcs that viewed humans solely as food or sex tools flooded into a city, the outcome was obvious.

I could say the option of quietly dealing with things from the shadows had now been crushed.

‘Still, openly allying with humans isn’t ideal either,’ I pondered.

I might have to revise my plans and settings a bit.

Currently, my cover story was: “The number of Orcs has grown too much, so I decided to thin them out. Give me detailed intel, and I’ll cull them to an appropriate level.”

If anyone questioned my reasoning, my excuse would be: “They’re gluttons; the more of them there are, the less food there is for me.”

I had thought that by rampaging in their rear lines, the human side would naturally push their frontlines forward, avoiding such a situation.

But from what I was hearing, the war situation was worse than expected.

The frontier villages had already been swallowed up. At one point, a city nearly fell; they barely managed to hold it with reinforcements and defended it successfully, but when they tried to go on the offensive afterward, they fell right into the Orcs’ trap, causing the front to collapse.

The representative woman told me with visible anger that she herself had been captured at that time, and that even while the armies kept pushing and pulling, quite a number of people were being abducted.

(Orcs using tactics… Could some new kind of variant have been born?)

Like Goblins, Orcs had command units, but it was hard to believe they could bring about results like this.

Still, it seemed the Furon side was aware of this too; at first, they had suspected sabotage by some other country, which delayed their response, and because of that, all contact with the northern frontier villages was lost—a blunder they bitterly regretted, clenching their fists.

(Well, how I move will depend on their response too…)

I would need to carefully review my plan and, depending on the situation, even consider lending a hand.

If possible, I wanted the countries carrying imperial blood to prosper.

Though I had become a monster, it might be a nation where the descendants of my family still lived.

A little favoritism should be acceptable.

Anyway, time passed as we kept walking.

As time passed, the sun set, and hunger set in.

Although I brought out water from the fortress, there was nothing edible except meat, and given the possibility it was human flesh, I couldn’t bring it along.

So, I dashed off to secure some game.

Some of them expressed disapproval about me, their escort, leaving, but after I explained there were no Orcs nearby and that I would rush back at the first loud shout, and with the representative woman’s order, they reluctantly backed down.

In just about fifteen minutes, I caught two boars and returned, only to find everyone crawling on the ground, hiding themselves.

They weren’t as good as Orcs, but I had a decent amount of experience with smell detection as well.

After quickly locating them all, I butchered the boars and moved to an open area.

Detaching the iron plate from my backpack, I began cutting and preparing the meat to grill, when they stopped me.

“Smoke will rise! What are you thinking!?”

I continued cooking and replied with, “So what?”

Their fussing was so over the top that I held up a written note: “Even if 100 or 200 Orcs come, I can just drive them off, right?” and focused on getting the meat grilled just right.

By now, grilling meat had become a refined craft.

Even so, the women were panicking over the rising smoke, so I scribbled another line.

“You see, when they have unnecessary intelligence, if you kill a certain number of them quickly, the rest will flee in fear of dying. Even if thousands come, if I charge in and go berserk, they’ll scatter in terror.”

The representative woman glared at me bitterly as she read my memo.

“Don’t you understand that these Orcs aren’t normal!?”

To which my reply was:

“Don’t measure me with human standards.”

If they were trained like a military, then what would come here would be scouts.

There was no way they’d swarm over here in droves, nor would they abandon the front line to send thousands of troops.

By my estimation, a hundred at most would show up.

And if it was just that many, I could wipe them out instantly if I went all out.

While my goal was to protect them, killing all Orcs before I even had to defend them was child’s play.

I tried to convey that again, but the representative still seemed unconvinced, and the other women trembled with fear.

(You’re soldiers, aren’t you? Pull yourselves together.)

Maybe as a man I couldn’t understand the trauma these women had suffered, but I wanted them to give their all to survive.

In the end, no one said a word about me sprinkling salt and pepper on the meat and grilling it on the iron plate, and the smoke issue was settled with the representative’s comment, “It’s already dark, so it should be fine.”

Though I wasn’t fully convinced, I reminded myself that I looked like a monster and thought, ‘At least they quieted down, so that’s good enough.’

There was no further mention of it during cleanup either, and feeling a little downhearted, I scrubbed the iron plate with a brush.

If anything, I felt accusing gazes saying, “Don’t waste precious water on that,” which made the atmosphere awkward.

Then we resumed our march, slowly walking through the forest in darkness for about two more hours before calling it a day.

Sleeping there was fine, but without a light source, everything was clumsy and disorganized.

They couldn’t even secure sleeping spots properly; I had to lend my night vision to get things barely sorted.

‘Are these people really soldiers?’ I thought, only to learn that except for the representative, some were recruits or hadn’t even completed training.

“You probably wouldn’t understand, but a lot of these kids were urgently drafted before they could finish combat training. So their mindset as soldiers is still incomplete. Maybe if you look at them strictly as fighters, they’re unqualified, but even kids like these have to go to the battlefield now.”

Apparently, the anti-Orc front was in a worse state than I had imagined, and the representative explained it apologetically, perhaps sensing my sigh.

Though her tone had a few thorns, there was no doubt she grieved over the current situation.

They said they would take turns keeping watch, but given their abilities and the circumstances, there was no way I could trust that; even when I gestured, “I’ll do it, you all sleep,” they wouldn’t agree.

In this pitch-black forest, it was questionable how effective watch duty by soldiers-in-training could be, but perhaps what they needed was simply the reassurance that “someone is watching.”

I let out a deep sigh as I watched half of them unable to sleep and the other half taking turns standing meaningless guard.

It looked like it was going to be a long night.

By the way, the reason I didn’t get aroused by their completely naked bodies was that my standards had gotten too high.

I already had a “model sister,” so I had always been strict when it came to judging women’s looks and figures.

On top of that, after spending days admiring the flawless naked body of that Dame Oppai woman, and seeing No. 6’s divine figure so many times that only “beautiful” could describe it, my eyes had become spoiled.

That’s why I behaved like a perfect gentleman toward them, but still, I did start craving some skin exposure.

If I managed to deliver them safely, I’d like to request some kind of “service,” though I kept watch, hoping a good idea would come to me.

+++
